      <p><b>Saint Patrick's Day</b>, or the <b>Feast of Saint Patrick</b> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga">Lá Fhéile Pádraig</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'the Day of the Festival of Patrick'), is a cultural and religious celebration held on 17 March, the traditional death date of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 385</span>&nbsp;– c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 461</span>), the foremost <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>. </p>
      <p>Saint Patrick's Day was made an official Christian <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">feast day</a> in the early 17th century and is observed by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a> (especially the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran Church</a>. The day commemorates Saint Patrick and the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Ireland" title="Christianity in Ireland">Christianity in Ireland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Arrival_of_Christianity_in_Ireland_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arrival_of_Christianity_in_Ireland-5">[5]</a></sup> and celebrates the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Ireland" title="Culture of Ireland">heritage and culture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">the Irish</a> in general.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_1_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_1-7">[7]</a></sup> Celebrations generally involve public parades and festivals, <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9il%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Céilí">céilís</a>, and the wearing of green attire or <a href="/wiki/Shamrock" title="Shamrock">shamrocks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_2_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_2-8">[8]</a></sup> Christians who belong to liturgical denominations also attend <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church services</a><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_1_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_5_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_5-9">[9]</a></sup> and historically the <a href="/wiki/Lenten" class="mw-redirect" title="Lenten">Lenten</a> restrictions <a href="/wiki/Christian_fasting" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian fasting">on eating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_alcohol" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianity and alcohol">drinking alcohol</a> were lifted for the day, which has encouraged and propagated the holiday's tradition of alcohol consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_1_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_2_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_2-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_3_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_3-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_4_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_4-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Saint Patrick's Day is a <a href="/wiki/Public_holiday" title="Public holiday">public holiday</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a> (for provincial government employees), and the British Overseas Territory of <a href="/wiki/Montserrat" title="Montserrat">Montserrat</a>. It is also widely celebrated in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, especially amongst <a href="/wiki/Irish_diaspora" title="Irish diaspora">Irish diaspora</a>. Saint Patrick's Day is celebrated in more countries than any other <a href="/wiki/National_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="National Day">national festival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002242_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002242-15">[15]</a></sup> Modern celebrations have been greatly influenced by those of the Irish diaspora, particularly those that developed in North America. However, there has been criticism of Saint Patrick's Day celebrations for having become too commercialised and for fostering negative stereotypes of the Irish people.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>

          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#South_America"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext">South America</span></a>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Saint_Patrick">Saint Patrick</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Saint Patrick">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Saint Patrick was a 5th-century Romano-British Christian missionary and Bishop in Ireland. Much of what is known about Saint Patrick comes from the <i>Declaration</i>, which was allegedly written by Patrick himself. It is believed that he was born in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a> in the fourth century, into a wealthy <a href="/wiki/Romano-British" class="mw-redirect" title="Romano-British">Romano-British</a> family. His father was a <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a> and his grandfather was a priest in the Christian church. According to the <i>Declaration</i>, at the age of sixteen, he was kidnapped by Irish raiders and taken as a slave to <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It says that he spent six years there working as a shepherd and that during this time he found God. The <i>Declaration</i> says that God told Patrick to flee to the coast, where a ship would be waiting to take him home. After making his way home, Patrick went on to become a priest.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>According to tradition, Patrick returned to Ireland to convert the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic polytheism">pagan</a> Irish to Christianity. The <i>Declaration</i> says that he spent many years evangelising in the northern half of Ireland and converted thousands. </p>
      <p>Patrick's efforts against the druids were eventually turned into an allegory in which he drove "snakes" out of Ireland, despite the fact that snakes were not known to inhabit the region.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Tradition holds that he died on 17 March and was buried at <a href="/wiki/Downpatrick" title="Downpatrick">Downpatrick</a>. Over the following centuries, many legends grew up around Patrick and he became Ireland's foremost saint.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Patrick is <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">remembered</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Festival_(Anglicanism)" title="Lesser Festival (Anglicanism)">Lesser Festival</a> on <a href="/wiki/March_17" title="March 17">17 March</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebration_and_traditions">Celebration and traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Celebration and traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg/220px-Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="73" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg/330px-Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg/440px-Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="334"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Traditional-irish-stipatricksidayibadges.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Traditional St Patrick's Day badges from the early 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Country_Life" class="mw-redirect" title="Museum of Country Life">Museum of Country Life</a> in <a href="/wiki/County_Mayo" title="County Mayo">County Mayo</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Irish_clover.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Irish_clover.jpg/170px-Irish_clover.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Irish_clover.jpg/255px-Irish_clover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Irish_clover.jpg 2x" data-file-width="304" data-file-height="302"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Irish_clover.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>According to legend, Saint Patrick used the three-leaved <a href="/wiki/Shamrock" title="Shamrock">shamrock</a> to explain the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Trinity" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Trinity">Holy Trinity</a> to Irish pagans.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Today's Saint Patrick's Day celebrations have been greatly influenced by those that developed among the Irish diaspora, especially in North America. Until the late 20th century, Saint Patrick's Day was often a bigger celebration among the diaspora than it was in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002242_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002242-15">[15]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Celebrations generally involve public parades and festivals, Irish traditional music sessions (<a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9ilidh" class="mw-redirect" title="Céilidh">céilithe</a>), and the wearing of green attire or shamrocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_2_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_2-8">[8]</a></sup> There are also formal gatherings such as banquets and dances, although these were more common in the past. Saint Patrick's Day parades began in North America in the 18th century but did not spread to Ireland until the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002xxiii_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002xxiii-20">[20]</a></sup> The participants generally include marching bands, the military, fire brigades, cultural organisations, charitable organisations, <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_association" title="Voluntary association">voluntary associations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Youth_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth organization">youth groups</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraternity" title="Fraternity">fraternities</a>, and so on. However, over time, many of the parades have become more akin to a <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">carnival</a>. More effort is made to use the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish language</a>, especially in Ireland, where the 1st March to St Patrick's Day on 17th March is Seachtain na Gaeilge ("<a href="/wiki/Seachtain_na_Gaeilge" title="Seachtain na Gaeilge">Irish language week</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2010, famous landmarks have been lit up in green on Saint Patrick's Day as part of <a href="/wiki/Tourism_Ireland" title="Tourism Ireland">Tourism Ireland</a>'s "Global Greening Initiative" or "Going Green for St Patrick's Day".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Opera_House" title="Sydney Opera House">Sydney Opera House</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sky_Tower_(Auckland)" title="Sky Tower (Auckland)">Sky Tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/Auckland" title="Auckland">Auckland</a> were the first landmarks to participate and since then over 300 landmarks in fifty countries across the globe have gone green for Saint Patricks day.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Christians may also attend <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church services</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_1_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_5_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_5-9">[9]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Lenten" class="mw-redirect" title="Lenten">Lenten</a> restrictions <a href="/wiki/Christian_fasting" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian fasting">on eating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_alcohol" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianity and alcohol">drinking alcohol</a> are lifted for the day. Perhaps because of this, drinking alcohol – particularly Irish whiskey, beer, or cider – has become an integral part of the celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_1_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_2_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_2-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_3_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_3-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Culture_4_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Culture_4-11">[11]</a></sup> The Saint Patrick's Day custom of "drowning the shamrock" or "wetting the shamrock" was historically popular. At the end of the celebrations, especially in Ireland. At the end of the celebrations, a shamrock is put into the bottom of a cup, which is then filled with whiskey, beer, or cider. It is then drunk as a <a href="/wiki/Toast_(honor)" title="Toast (honor)">toast</a> to Saint Patrick, Ireland, or those present. The shamrock would either be swallowed with the drink or taken out and tossed over the shoulder for good luck. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair200226_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair200226-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Irish Government Ministers travel abroad on official visits to various countries around the globe to celebrate Saint Patrick's Day and promote Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The most prominent of these is the visit of the Irish <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a> (Irish Prime Minister) with the <a href="/wiki/U.S._President" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. President">U.S. President</a> which happens on or around Saint Patrick's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes.com_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes.com-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Traditionally the Taoiseach presents the U.S. President a <a href="/wiki/Waterford_Crystal" title="Waterford Crystal">Waterford Crystal</a> bowl filled with shamrocks.<sup id="cite_ref-irishexaminer.com_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishexaminer.com-33">[33]</a></sup> This tradition began when in 1952, Irish Ambassador to the U.S. <a href="/wiki/John_Hearne_(lawyer)" title="John Hearne (lawyer)">John Hearne</a> sent a box of shamrocks to <a href="/wiki/President_Harry_S._Truman" class="mw-redirect" title="President Harry S. Truman">President Harry S. Truman</a>. From then on it became an annual tradition of the Irish ambassador to the U.S. to present the Saint Patrick's Day shamrock to an official in the U.S. President's administration, although on some occasions the shamrock presentation was made by the Irish Taoiseach or Irish President to the U.S. President personally in Washington, such as when <a href="/wiki/President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="President Dwight D. Eisenhower">President Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> met <a href="/wiki/John_A._Costello" title="John A. Costello">Taoiseach John A. Costello</a> in 1956 and <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_T._O%27Kelly" title="Seán T. O'Kelly">President Seán T. O'Kelly</a> in 1959 or when <a href="/wiki/President_Ronald_Reagan" class="mw-redirect" title="President Ronald Reagan">President Ronald Reagan</a> met <a href="/wiki/Garret_FitzGerald" title="Garret FitzGerald">Taoiseach Garret FitzGerald</a> in 1986 and <a href="/wiki/Charles_J._Haughey" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles J. Haughey">Taoiseach Charles J. Haughey</a> in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes.com_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes.com-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-irishexaminer.com_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishexaminer.com-33">[33]</a></sup> However it was only after the meeting between <a href="/wiki/Albert_Reynolds" title="Albert Reynolds">Taoiseach Albert Reynolds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">President Bill Clinton</a> in 1994 that the presenting of the shamrock ceremony became an annual event for the leaders of both countries for Saint Patrick's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-irishtimes.com_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes.com-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The presenting of the Shamrock ceremony was cancelled in 2020 due to the severity of the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wearing_green">Wearing green</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wearing green">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg/170px-Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg/255px-Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg/340px-Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2468" data-file-height="3701"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Totally_Irish_in_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Women wearing green in London
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On Saint Patrick's Day, it is customary to wear <a href="/wiki/Shamrock" title="Shamrock">shamrocks</a>, green clothing or green accessories. Saint Patrick is said to have used the shamrock, a three-leaved plant, to explain the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Trinity" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Trinity">Holy Trinity</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic polytheism">pagan Irish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-natgeo_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This story first appears in writing in 1726, though it may be older. In pagan Ireland, three was a significant number and the Irish had many <a href="/wiki/Triple_deity" title="Triple deity">triple deities</a>, a fact that may have aided St Patrick in his <a href="/wiki/Evangelism" title="Evangelism">evangelisation</a> efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-Monaghan2009_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monaghan2009-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hegarty2012_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hegarty2012-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Monaghan" title="Patricia Monaghan">Patricia Monaghan</a> says there is no evidence that the shamrock was sacred to the pagan Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-Monaghan2009_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monaghan2009-39">[39]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Santino" title="Jack Santino">Jack Santino</a> speculates that it may have represented the regenerative powers of nature, and was recast in a Christian context‍—‌<a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icons</a> of St Patrick often depict the saint "with a <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a> in one hand and a sprig of shamrocks in the other".<sup id="cite_ref-Santino1995_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santino1995-41">[41]</a></sup> Roger Homan writes, "We can perhaps see St Patrick drawing upon the visual concept of the <i><a href="/wiki/Triple_spiral" class="mw-redirect" title="Triple spiral">triskele</a></i> when he uses the shamrock to explain the Trinity".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first association of the colour green with Ireland is from the 11th century pseudo-historical book <i><a href="/wiki/Lebor_Gab%C3%A1la_%C3%89renn" title="Lebor Gabála Érenn">Lebor Gabála Érenn</a></i> (The Book of the Taking of Ireland), which forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Mythological_Cycle" title="Mythological Cycle">Mythological Cycle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Irish_Mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Mythology">Irish Mythology</a> and describes the story of <a href="/wiki/Go%C3%ADdel_Glas" title="Goídel Glas">Goídel Glas</a> who is credited as the <a href="/wiki/Eponymous_ancestor" class="mw-redirect" title="Eponymous ancestor">eponymous ancestor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a> and creator of the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2005_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2005-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacKillop2005_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKillop2005-44">[44]</a></sup> In the story Goídel Glas, who was the son of <a href="/wiki/Scota" title="Scota">Scota</a> and Niul, was bitten by a snake and was saved from death by <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> placing his staff on the snakebite. As a reminder of the incident he would retain a green mark that would stay with him and he would lead his people to a land that would be free of snakes.<sup id="cite_ref-Macalister1939_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macalister1939-45">[45]</a></sup> This is emphasized in his name Goídel which was anglicised to the word Gaelic and Glas which is the Irish word for green.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2005_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2005-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacKillop2005_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKillop2005-44">[44]</a></sup> Another story from the <a href="/wiki/Lebor_Gab%C3%A1la_%C3%89renn" title="Lebor Gabála Érenn">Lebor Gabála Érenn</a> written after the adventures of <a href="/wiki/Go%C3%ADdel_Glas" title="Goídel Glas">Goídel Glas</a> refers to <a href="/wiki/Milesians_(Irish)#Myth" title="Milesians (Irish)">Íth</a> climbing the tower (in reference to the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Hercules" title="Tower of Hercules">Tower of Hercules</a>) his father <a href="/wiki/Breog%C3%A1n" title="Breogán">Breogán</a> builds in Brigantia (modern day <a href="/wiki/A_Coru%C3%B1a" title="A Coruña">Corunna</a> in <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>) on a winters day and is so captivated by the sight of a beautiful green island in the distance that he must set sail immediately. This story also introduces three national personifications of Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Banba" title="Banba">Banba</a>, <a href="/wiki/F%C3%B3dla" title="Fódla">Fódla</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89riu" title="Ériu">Ériu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2005_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2005-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacKillop2005_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKillop2005-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Macalister1939_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macalister1939-45">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The colour green was further associated with Ireland from the 1640s, when the <a href="/wiki/Flag_and_coat_of_arms_of_Leinster" class="mw-redirect" title="Flag and coat of arms of Leinster">green harp flag</a> was used by the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Ireland" title="Confederate Ireland">Irish Catholic Confederation</a>. <a href="/wiki/James_Connolly" title="James Connolly">James Connolly</a> would later describe this flag. prior to the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">1916 Easter Rising</a>, as representing "the sacred emblem of Ireland's unconquered soul".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Green ribbons and shamrocks have been worn on St Patrick's Day since at least the 1680s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002-47">[47]</a></sup> The Friendly Brothers of St Patrick, an Irish <a href="/wiki/Fraternity" title="Fraternity">fraternity</a> founded in about 1750,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> adopted green as its colour.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> However, when the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Patrick" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of St. Patrick">Order of St. Patrick</a>—an Anglo-Irish <a href="/wiki/Chivalric_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Chivalric order">chivalric order</a>—was founded in 1783 it adopted blue as its colour, which led to blue <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick%27s_blue" title="St. Patrick's blue">being associated with St Patrick</a>. During the 1790s, green would become associated with <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">Irish nationalism</a>, due to its use by the <a href="/wiki/United_Irishmen" class="mw-redirect" title="United Irishmen">United Irishmen</a>. This was a <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">republican</a> organisation—led mostly by Protestants but with many Catholic members—who launched a <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">rebellion in 1798</a> against British rule. Ireland was described as "the Emerald Isle" for the first time in print in "When Erin First Rose" (1795), a poem by co-founder of the United Irishmen <a href="/wiki/William_Drennan" title="William Drennan">William Drennan</a>, which stresses the historical importance of green to the Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The phrase "wearing of the green" comes from a <a href="/wiki/The_Wearing_of_the_Green" title="The Wearing of the Green">song of the same name</a>, which laments United Irishmen supporters being persecuted for wearing green. The late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries have seen the re-emergence of Irish cultural symbols, such as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Language">Irish Language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_mythology" title="Irish mythology">Irish mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_folklore" title="Irish folklore">Irish folklore</a>, and the colour green, through the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic Revival">Gaelic Revival</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Literary_Revival" title="Irish Literary Revival">Irish Literary Revival</a> which served to stir Irish nationalist sentiment. The influence of green was more prominently observable in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_flags_of_Ireland#Political_flags" title="List of flags of Ireland">flags</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">1916 Easter Rising</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Sunburst_flag" title="Sunburst flag">Sunburst flag</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Starry_Plough_(flag)" title="Starry Plough (flag)">Starry Plough Banner</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republic" title="Irish Republic">Proclamation Flag of the Irish Republic</a> which was flown over the <a href="/wiki/General_Post_Office,_Dublin" title="General Post Office, Dublin">General Post Office, Dublin</a> together with the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ireland" title="Flag of Ireland">Irish Tricolour</a>. When Ireland did achieve its independence in 1922, the first act by the new <i><a href="/wiki/Saorst%C3%A1t_%C3%89ireann" class="mw-redirect" title="Saorstát Éireann">Saorstát Éireann</a></i> (Irish Free State) government was to order all the <a href="/wiki/Pillar_box#Ireland" title="Pillar box">post boxes</a> to be painted 'Saorstát green' (as it was described), under the slogan "Green paint for a green people";<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson2016_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson2016-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> in 1924, the government introduced a green <a href="/wiki/Irish_passport" title="Irish passport">Irish passport</a> for Irish citizens, and it would remain this colour until the introduction of the burgundy coloured <a href="/wiki/Passports_of_the_European_Union" title="Passports of the European Union">European Passports</a> in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EUED_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUED-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EURes1981_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EURes1981-58">[58]</a></sup> Throughout these centuries, the colour green and its association with St Patrick's Day grew.<sup id="cite_ref-history.com_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history.com-59">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The wearing of the 'St Patrick's Day Cross' was also a popular custom in Ireland until the early 20th century. These were a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_cross" title="Celtic cross">Celtic Christian cross</a> made of paper that was "covered with silk or ribbon of different colours, and a bunch or rosette of green silk in the centre".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair200225–26_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair200225–26-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Celebrations_by_region">Celebrations by region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Celebrations by region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St._Patricks_Day_Parade_(2013)_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_(8566201364).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/St._Patricks_Day_Parade_%282013%29_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_%288566201364%29.jpg/220px-St._Patricks_Day_Parade_%282013%29_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_%288566201364%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/St._Patricks_Day_Parade_%282013%29_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_%288566201364%29.jpg/330px-St._Patricks_Day_Parade_%282013%29_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_%288566201364%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/St._Patricks_Day_Parade_%282013%29_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_%288566201364%29.jpg/440px-St._Patricks_Day_Parade_%282013%29_In_Dublin_Was_Excellent_But_The_Weather_And_The_Turnout_Was_Disappointing_%288566201364%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000"></a>

        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_(16650284488).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_%2816650284488%29.jpg/220px-ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_%2816650284488%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_%2816650284488%29.jpg/330px-ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_%2816650284488%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_%2816650284488%29.jpg/440px-ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_%2816650284488%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5845" data-file-height="4000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:ST._PATRICK%27S_SPIRE_OF_LIGHT_ON_O%27CONNELL_STREET_IN_DUBLIN_REF-102056_(16650284488).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Dublin's <a href="/wiki/General_Post_Office,_Dublin" title="General Post Office, Dublin">General Post Office</a> and the Spire on O'Connell Street on St. Patrick's Day
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Saint Patrick's feast day, as a kind of national day, was already being celebrated by the Irish in Europe in the ninth and tenth centuries. In later times, he became more and more widely seen as the patron of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Saint Patrick's <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> was finally placed on the universal <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical calendar">liturgical calendar</a> in the Catholic Church due to the influence of <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a>-born <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscan</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Luke_Wadding" title="Luke Wadding">Luke Wadding</a><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> in the early 1600s. Saint Patrick's Day thus became a <a href="/wiki/Holy_day_of_obligation" title="Holy day of obligation">holy day of obligation</a> for Roman Catholics in Ireland. It is also a feast day in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>, which is part of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>. The church calendar avoids the observance of saints' feasts during certain solemnities, moving the saint's day to a time outside those periods. St Patrick's Day is occasionally affected by this requirement, when 17 March falls during <a href="/wiki/Holy_Week" title="Holy Week">Holy Week</a>. This happened in 1940, when Saint Patrick's Day was observed on 3 April to avoid it coinciding with <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a>, and again in 2008, where it was officially observed on 15 March.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> St Patrick's Day will not fall within Holy Week again until 2160.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> However, the popular festivities may still be held on 17 March or on a weekend near to the feast day.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In 1903, St Patrick's Day became an official public holiday in Ireland. This was thanks to the <a href="/wiki/Bank_Holiday" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank Holiday">Bank Holiday</a> (Ireland) Act 1903, an act of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Parliament">United Kingdom Parliament</a> introduced by Irish Member of Parliament <a href="/wiki/James_O%27Mara" title="James O'Mara">James O'Mara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-omara1_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omara1-66">[66]</a></sup> O'Mara later introduced the law which required that <a href="/wiki/Public_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Public house">public houses</a> be shut on 17 March, a provision that was later deemed unnecessary and was repealed in the 1970s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The first St Patrick's Day parade in Ireland was held in <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a> in 1903. The week of St Patrick's Day 1903 had been declared Irish Language Week by the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic League">Gaelic League</a> and in Waterford they opted to have a procession on Sunday 15 March. The procession comprised the Mayor and members of Waterford Corporation, the Trades Hall, the various trade unions and bands who included the 'Barrack St Band' and the '<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Francis_Meagher" title="Thomas Francis Meagher">Thomas Francis Meagher</a> Band'.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The parade began at the premises of the Gaelic League in George's St and finished in the Peoples Park, where the public were addressed by the Mayor and other dignitaries.<sup id="cite_ref-munster_express_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-munster_express-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> On Tuesday 17 March, most Waterford businesses—including public houses—were closed and marching bands paraded as they had two days previously.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The Waterford Trades Hall had been emphatic that the National Holiday be observed.<sup id="cite_ref-munster_express_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-munster_express-68">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On St Patrick's Day 1916, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a>—an Irish nationalist paramilitary organisation—held parades throughout Ireland. The authorities recorded 38 St Patrick's Day parades, involving 6,000 marchers, almost half of whom were said to be armed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002105_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002105-71">[71]</a></sup> The following month, the Irish Volunteers launched the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> against British rule. This marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_revolutionary_period" title="Irish revolutionary period">Irish revolutionary period</a> and led to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">Civil War</a>. During this time, St Patrick's Day celebrations in Ireland were muted, although the day was sometimes chosen to hold large political rallies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002108_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002108-72">[72]</a></sup> The celebrations remained low-key after the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>; the only state-organized observance was a military procession and <a href="/wiki/Trooping_the_colours" class="mw-redirect" title="Trooping the colours">trooping of the colours</a>, and an Irish-language <a href="/wiki/Mass_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass (Catholic Church)">mass</a> attended by government ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002134_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002134-73">[73]</a></sup> In 1927, the Irish Free State government <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">banned the selling of alcohol</a> on St Patrick's Day, although it remained legal in Northern Ireland. The ban was not repealed until 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002135–136_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002135–136-74">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The first official, state-sponsored St Patrick's Day parade in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> took place in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On three occasions, parades across the Republic of Ireland have been cancelled from taking place on St Patrick's Day, with all years involving health and safety reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In 2001, as a precaution to the <a href="/wiki/2001_United_Kingdom_foot-and-mouth_outbreak" title="2001 United Kingdom foot-and-mouth outbreak">foot-and-mouth outbreak</a>, St Patrick's Day celebrations were postponed to May<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and in 2020 and 2021, as a consequence to the severity of the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>, the St Patrick's Day Parade was cancelled outright.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Organisers of the St Patrick's Day Festival 2021 will instead host virtual events around Ireland on their SPF TV online channel.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession,_Downpatrick,_March_2010_(03).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession%2C_Downpatrick%2C_March_2010_%2803%29.JPG/220px-St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession%2C_Downpatrick%2C_March_2010_%2803%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession%2C_Downpatrick%2C_March_2010_%2803%29.JPG/330px-St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession%2C_Downpatrick%2C_March_2010_%2803%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession%2C_Downpatrick%2C_March_2010_%2803%29.JPG/440px-St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession%2C_Downpatrick%2C_March_2010_%2803%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:St_Patricks_Day_Inter_Church_Procession,_Downpatrick,_March_2010_(03).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A St Patrick's Day religious procession in <a href="/wiki/Downpatrick" title="Downpatrick">Downpatrick</a>, where Saint Patrick is said to be buried
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, the celebration of St Patrick's Day was affected by sectarian divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177-88">[88]</a></sup> A majority of the population were Protestant <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">Ulster unionists</a> who saw themselves as British, while a substantial minority were Catholic Irish nationalists who saw themselves as Irish. Although it was a public holiday, Northern Ireland's unionist government did not officially observe St Patrick's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177-88">[88]</a></sup> During the conflict known as <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a> (late 1960s–late 1990s), public St Patrick's Day celebrations were rare and tended to be associated with the Catholic community.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177-88">[88]</a></sup> In 1976, <a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalism" title="Ulster loyalism">loyalists</a> <a href="/wiki/Hillcrest_Bar_bombing" title="Hillcrest Bar bombing">detonated a car bomb</a> outside a pub crowded with Catholics celebrating St Patrick's Day in <a href="/wiki/Dungannon" title="Dungannon">Dungannon</a>; four civilians were killed and many injured. However, some Protestant unionists attempted to 're-claim' the festival, and in 1985 the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Order" title="Orange Order">Orange Order</a> held its own St Patrick's Day parade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177-88">[88]</a></sup> Since the end of the conflict in 1998 there have been cross-community St Patrick's Day parades in towns throughout Northern Ireland, which have attracted thousands of spectators.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177_88-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECroninAdair2002175–177-88">[88]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the mid-1990s the government of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> began a campaign to use St Patrick's Day to showcase Ireland and its culture.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-89">[89]</a></sup> The government set up a group called St Patrick's Festival, with the aims: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
       <ul>
        <li>To offer a national festival that ranks amongst all of the greatest celebrations in the world</li> 
        <li>To create energy and excitement throughout Ireland via innovation, creativity, grassroots involvement, and marketing activity</li> 
        <li>To provide the opportunity and motivation for people of Irish descent (and those who sometimes wish they were Irish) to attend and join in the imaginative and expressive celebrations</li> 
        <li>To project, internationally, an accurate image of Ireland as a creative, professional and sophisticated country with wide appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-festival_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-festival-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
       </ul> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The first St Patrick's Festival was held on 17 March 1996. In 1997, it became a three-day event, and by 2000 it was a four-day event. By 2006, the festival was five days long; more than 675,000 people attended the 2009 parade. Overall 2009's five-day festival saw almost 1&nbsp;million visitors, who took part in festivities that included concerts, outdoor theatre performances, and fireworks.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Skyfest" title="Skyfest">Skyfest</a> which ran from 2006 to 2012 formed the centrepiece of the St Patrick's festival.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The topic of the 2004 St Patrick's Symposium was "Talking Irish", during which the nature of Irish identity, economic success, and the future were discussed. Since 1996, there has been a greater emphasis on celebrating and projecting a fluid and inclusive notion of "Irishness" rather than an identity based around traditional religious or ethnic allegiance. The week around St Patrick's Day usually involves <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish language</a> speakers using more Irish during <i><a href="/wiki/Seachtain_na_Gaeilge" title="Seachtain na Gaeilge">Seachtain na Gaeilge</a></i> ("Irish Language Week").<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Christian leaders in Ireland have expressed concern about the secularisation of St Patrick's Day. In <i>The Word</i> magazine's March 2007 issue, Fr <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Twomey" title="Vincent Twomey">Vincent Twomey</a> wrote, "It is time to reclaim St Patrick's Day as a church festival". He questioned the need for "mindless alcohol-fuelled revelry" and concluded that "it is time to bring the piety and the fun together".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As well as Dublin, many other cities, towns, and villages in Ireland hold their own parades and festivals, including <a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>, Derry, <a href="/wiki/Galway" title="Galway">Galway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kilkenny" title="Kilkenny">Kilkenny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limerick" title="Limerick">Limerick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The biggest celebrations outside the cities are in <a href="/wiki/Downpatrick" title="Downpatrick">Downpatrick</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">County Down</a>, where Saint Patrick is said to be buried. The shortest St. Patrick's Day parade in the world formerly took place in <a href="/wiki/Dripsey" title="Dripsey">Dripsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">County Cork</a>. The parade lasted just 23.4 metres and traveled between the village's two pubs. The annual event began in 1999, but ceased after five years when one of the two pubs closed.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elsewhere_in_Europe">Elsewhere in Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Elsewhere in Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg/220px-St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg/330px-St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg/440px-St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3025" data-file-height="1683"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:St_Patrick%27s_Day_-_Trafalgar_Square_March_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Saint Patrick's Day celebration at <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> in London, 2006
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In England, the <a href="/wiki/British_royal_family" title="British royal family">British Royals</a> traditionally present bowls of shamrock to members of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Guards" title="Irish Guards">Irish Guards</a>, a regiment in the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>, following <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_of_Denmark" title="Alexandra of Denmark">Queen Alexandra</a> introducing the tradition in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Since 2012 the <a href="/wiki/Catherine,_Duchess_of_Cambridge" title="Catherine, Duchess of Cambridge">Duchess of Cambridge</a> has presented the bowls of shamrock to the Irish Guards. While female royals are often tasked with presenting the bowls of shamrock, male royals have also undertaking the role, such as <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">King George VI</a> in 1950 to mark the 50th anniversary of the formation of the Irish Guards, and in 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Prince_William,_Duke_of_Cambridge" title="Prince William, Duke of Cambridge">Duke of Cambridge</a> in place of his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Fresh Shamrocks are presented to the Irish Guards, regardless of where they are stationed, and are flown in from Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While some Saint Patrick's Day celebrations could be conducted openly in Britain pre 1960s, this would change following the commencement by the IRA's bombing campaign on <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">mainland Britain</a> and as a consequence this resulted in a suspicion of all things Irish and those who supported them which led to people of Irish descent wearing a sprig of shamrock on Saint Patrick's day in private or attending specific events.<sup id="cite_ref-Pages_180–183_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pages_180–183-102">[102]</a></sup> Today after many years following <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">the Good Friday Agreement</a>, people of Irish descent openly wear a sprig of shamrock to celebrate their Irishness.<sup id="cite_ref-Pages_180–183_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pages_180–183-102">[102]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Christian denominations in Great Britain observing his <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> include <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="The Church of England">The Church of England</a> and the Roman Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_and_non-Irish_celebrants_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_and_non-Irish_celebrants-103">[103]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> holds the largest Saint Patrick's Day parade in Britain with a city centre parade<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> over a two-mile (3&nbsp;km) route through the city centre. The organisers describe it as the third biggest parade in the world after Dublin and New York.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>London, since 2002, has had an annual Saint Patrick's Day parade which takes place on weekends around the 17th, usually in Trafalgar Square. In 2008 the water in the Trafalgar Square fountains was dyed green. In 2020 the Parade was cancelled due to the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> has the highest proportion of residents with Irish ancestry of any English city.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> This has led to a long-standing celebration on St Patrick's Day in terms of music, cultural events and the parade.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> hosts a two-week Irish festival in the weeks prior to Saint Patrick's Day. The festival includes an Irish Market based at the city's town hall which flies the Irish tricolour opposite the Union Flag, a large parade as well as a large number of cultural and learning events throughout the two-week period.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malta">Malta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Malta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg/220px-Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg/330px-Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg/440px-Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Porte_des_Bombes_on_St_Patrick%27s_Night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Porte_des_Bombes" class="mw-redirect" title="Porte des Bombes">Porte des Bombes</a> illuminated in green on Saint Patrick's Day of 2014
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first Saint Patrick's Day celebrations in Malta took place in the early 20th century by soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dublin_Fusiliers" title="Royal Dublin Fusiliers">Royal Dublin Fusiliers</a> who were stationed in <a href="/wiki/Floriana" title="Floriana">Floriana</a>. Celebrations were held in the Balzunetta area of the town, which contained a number of bars and was located close to the barracks. The Irish diaspora in Malta continued to celebrate the feast annually.<sup id="cite_ref-maltaindependent2018-03-18_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maltaindependent2018-03-18-108">[108]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Today, Saint Patrick's Day is mainly celebrated in Spinola Bay and <a href="/wiki/Paceville" title="Paceville">Paceville</a> areas of <a href="/wiki/St_Julian%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="St Julian's">St Julian's</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> although other celebrations still occur at Floriana<sup id="cite_ref-maltaindependent2018-03-18_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-maltaindependent2018-03-18-108">[108]</a></sup> and other locations.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Thousands of Maltese attend the celebrations, "which are more associated with drinking beer than traditional Irish culture."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>Norway has had a St. Patrick's Day parade in Oslo since 2000, first organized by Irish expatriates living in Norway, and partially coordinated with the Irish embassy in Oslo.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg/220px-St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg/330px-St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg/440px-St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:St_Patrick%27s_Day_2012_in_Moscow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> hosts an annual Saint Patrick's Day festival.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first Saint Patrick's Day parade in Russia took place in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Since 1999, there has been a yearly "Saint Patrick's Day" festival in Moscow and other Russian cities.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The official part of the Moscow parade is a military-style parade and is held in collaboration with the Moscow government and the Irish embassy in Moscow. The unofficial parade is held by volunteers and resembles a carnival. In 2014, Moscow Irish Week was celebrated from 12 to 23 March, which includes Saint Patrick's Day on 17 March. Over 70 events celebrating Irish culture in Moscow, St Petersburg, Yekaterinburg, Voronezh, and Volgograd were sponsored by the Irish Embassy, the Moscow City Government, and other organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> added the feast day of Saint Patrick to <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_calendar" title="Eastern Orthodox liturgical calendar">its liturgical calendar</a>, to be celebrated on 30 March&nbsp;[<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 17 March].<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bosnia_and_Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bosnia and Herzegovina">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a>, the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> has a large Irish expatriate community.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The community established the <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo_Irish_Festival" title="Sarajevo Irish Festival">Sarajevo Irish Festival</a> in 2015, which is held for three days around and including Saint Patrick's Day. The festival organizes an annual a parade, hosts Irish theatre companies, screens Irish films and organizes concerts of Irish folk musicians. The festival has hosted numerous Irish artists, filmmakers, theatre directors and musicians such as <a href="/wiki/Conor_Horgan" title="Conor Horgan">Conor Horgan</a>, Ailis Ni Riain, <a href="/wiki/Dermot_Dunne" title="Dermot Dunne">Dermot Dunne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mick_Moloney" title="Mick Moloney">Mick Moloney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlo%C3%AB_Agnew" title="Chloë Agnew">Chloë Agnew</a> and others.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG/220px-Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG/330px-Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG/440px-Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1776" data-file-height="1035"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Stpatricksdayfestivalcbridge.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>2009 <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick%27s_Day_festival_Coatbridge" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Patrick's Day festival Coatbridge">Saint Patrick's Day festival</a> celebration in <a href="/wiki/Coatbridge" title="Coatbridge">Coatbridge</a>, Scotland
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Scottish town of <a href="/wiki/Coatbridge" title="Coatbridge">Coatbridge</a>, where the majority of the town's population are of Irish descent,<sup id="cite_ref-ScotGov-Immigration_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScotGov-Immigration-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CoatbridgeFestival_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoatbridgeFestival-125">[125]</a></sup> also has <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick%27s_Day_festival_Coatbridge" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Patrick's Day festival Coatbridge">a Saint Patrick's Day Festival</a> which includes celebrations and parades in the town centre.<sup id="cite_ref-CoatbridgeFestival_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoatbridgeFestival-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> has a considerably large Irish population; due, for the most part, to the Irish immigration during the 19th century. This immigration was the main cause in raising the population of Glasgow by over 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Due to this large Irish population, there are many Irish-themed pubs and Irish interest groups who hold yearly celebrations on Saint Patrick's day in Glasgow. Glasgow has held a yearly Saint Patrick's Day parade and festival since 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>While Saint Patrick's Day in Switzerland is commonly celebrated on 17 March with festivities similar to those in neighbouring central European countries, it is not unusual for Swiss students to organise celebrations in their own living spaces on Saint Patrick's Eve. Most popular are usually those in Zurich's <a href="/wiki/Aussersihl" title="Aussersihl">Kreis 4</a>. Traditionally, guests also contribute with beverages and dress in green.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithuania">Lithuania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lithuania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>Although it is not a national holiday in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vilnia_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilnia River">Vilnia River</a> is dyed green every year on the Saint Patrick's Day in the capital <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg/220px-Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg/330px-Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg/440px-Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1440" data-file-height="960"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Patricks_Day_in_Motomachi_Yokohama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Saint Patrick's Day in <a href="/wiki/Motomachi,_Yokohama" title="Motomachi, Yokohama">Motomachi, Yokohama</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Saint Patrick's parades are now held in many locations across Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-url2013_St_Patricks_Day_Parades_in_Japan_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-url2013_St_Patricks_Day_Parades_in_Japan-131">[131]</a></sup> The first parade, in Tokyo, was organised by The Irish Network Japan (INJ) in 1992. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The Irish Association of Korea has celebrated Saint Patrick's Day since 1976 in <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, the capital city of <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. The place of the parade and festival has been moved from <a href="/wiki/Itaewon" title="Itaewon">Itaewon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daehangno" title="Daehangno">Daehangno</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cheonggyecheon" title="Cheonggyecheon">Cheonggyecheon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the St Patrick's Society of Selangor, founded in 1925, organises a yearly St Patrick's Ball, described as the biggest Saint Patrick's Day celebration in Asia. <a href="/wiki/Guinness_Anchor_Berhad" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinness Anchor Berhad">Guinness Anchor Berhad</a> also organises 36 parties across the country in places like the <a href="/wiki/Klang_Valley" title="Klang Valley">Klang Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johor_Bahru" title="Johor Bahru">Johor Bahru</a>, Malacca, <a href="/wiki/Ipoh" title="Ipoh">Ipoh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuantan" title="Kuantan">Kuantan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kota_Kinabalu" title="Kota Kinabalu">Kota Kinabalu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miri,_Malaysia" class="mw-redirect" title="Miri, Malaysia">Miri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuching" title="Kuching">Kuching</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean">Caribbean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Caribbean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Montserrat">Montserrat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Patrick%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Montserrat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The island of <a href="/wiki/Montserrat" title="Montserrat">Montserrat</a> is known as the "Emerald Island of the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a>" because of its founding by <a href="/wiki/Irish_immigration_to_Saint_Kitts_and_Nevis" title="Irish immigration to Saint Kitts and Nevis">Irish refugees from Saint Kitts and Nevis</a>.
